The Preponderant and the First Objection
Framing and refutation of the first objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ashʿarī argument for divine coercion does not contradict the necessary knowledge of human power and choice.

Core Idea
The preponderant affirms choice; our proof negates independence, not power.

This lesson begins by establishing that voluntary acts require a preponderant cause (incentive) and that this does not negate human choice. It then addresses the first objection: that such an argument contradicts the universally known fact that humans have power and choice. The author demonstrates that the argument only denies the servant's independence in causing the act, not the existence of power and choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the framing passage
Pay attention to how the author defines the preponderant (murjīḥ) as an incentive (dāʿī) that necessitates choice and act, and note that this is the solution (ḥall).
2
Identify the first objection
The objector claims the argument contradicts necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī). Find the objection in the text.
3
Analyze the reply
The author distinguishes between the necessary existence of power/choice and the occurrence of the act by the servant's power. See how the proof only negates the latter.
This reply avoids contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات الأفعال الاختيارية، فإن حركة المرتعش والنائم والمغمى عليه لا توصف في الشرع بحسن ولا قبح ويستلزم أيضاً كون التكاليف بأسرها تكليفاً بما لا يطاق ولا قائل به وأيضاً فالمرجح الذي يتوقف عليه فعل العبد ( داع له يقتضي اختياره ) الموجب ( للفعل وذلك لا ينفي الاختيار ) بل يثبته وهذا السؤال هو الحل وما قبله إما نقض أو في حكمه . قلنا) أما الأول : فلان الضروري وجود القدرة ) والاختيار (لا وقوع الفعل بقدرته) واختياره واستدلالنا إنما هو على نفي الثاني دون الأول فلا يكون مصادماً للضرورة ، ( وأما الثاني : ( وهو النقض بأفعال الباري ( فالمقدمة القائلة بأن الفعل الواقع لا لمرجح اتفاقي لا اختياري ) إنما هي مقدمة إلزامية بالنسبة إلى ) المعتزلة ( القائلين بأن قدرة العبد لا تؤثر في فعل إلا إذا انضم إليها مرجح ) يسمونه الداعي ( ونحن لا نقول بها فإن الترجيح بمجرد الاختيار المتعلق بأحد طرفي الفعل لا لداع ( عندنا جائز ولا يخرج ذلك الفعل عن كونه اختبارياً كما تقدم في مسألة الهارب من السبع والعطشان الواجد للقدحين المتساويين. وإذا لم نقل بهذه المقدمة لم يرد علينا النقض بفعل الله تعالى ( وأيضاً ) على تقدير صحة هذه المقدمة عندنا ليس هذا الدليل بعينه جارياً في فعله تعالى لأنا نختار أنه متمكن من الترك وأن فعله يتوقف على مرجح لكن ذلك المرجح قديم فلا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر حتى يلزم التسلسل في المرجحات كما في فعل العبد إذا كان مرجحه صادرا عنه، إذ عنه، إذ لا بد أن يكون ذلك الصادر عنه حادثاً محتاجاً إلى آخر فالمقدمة القائلة بأن مرجح الفعل إذا كان صادراً عن فاعله لزم التسلسل غير صادقة في حقه تعالى بل في حق العبد، وإلى ما قررناه أشار بقوله : ( فمرجح فاعليته تعالى ( قديم هو إرادته وقدرته المستندتان إلى ذاته إيجاباً والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص فيكون اتفاقياً كما مر فى العبد . قلت : لنا أن نختار الوجوب ولا محذور لأن المرجح الموجب إرادته المستند إلى ذاته بخلاف إرادة العبد فإنها مستند إلى غير ذلك فإذا كانت موجبة لزم الجبر فيه قطعاً. وقد مر هذا مرة مع الإشارة إلى ما فيه من شائبة الإيجاب فلا يحتاج) ذلك المرجح القديم إلى مرجح آخر حتى يتسلسل إذ المحوج إلى المؤثر عندنا الحدوث دون الإمكان بخلاف مرجح فاعلية العبد فإنه حادث محتاج إلى مؤثر فإن كان مؤثره العبد تسلسل وإن كان غيره كان هو مجبوراً في فعله . ( وأما الثالث : ( وهو النقض بالحسن والقبح الشرعيين ( فلا يجب عندنا في الواجب الشرعي تأثير قدرة الفاعل فيه بل يجب أن يكون الفعل مما هو مقدور عادة) أي يكون مما يقارنه القدرة والاختيار في الجملة ولا يكفي ذلك في الواجب العقلي عندكم إذ لا بد فيه من تأثير القدرة فلا يتجه علينا النقض بالشرعي. ( وأما الرابع : ) وهو الحل ( فمقصودنا ) من دليلنا على كون العبد مجبوراً مضطراً ) أن العبد غير مستقل بإيجاد فعله من غير داع واختيار يترتب على ذلك الداعي ويوجب الفعل ( يحصل ) أي ذلك الداعي مع ما يترتب عليه ) له بخلق الله تعالى إياه، وقد بيناه ) عدم استقلاله بهذا المعنى ) وذلك كاف في عدم الحكم ) بالحسن و القبح ( عقلاً إذ لا فرق بين (الفعل) في العبد كما قاله الشيخ، وبين يوجد ما يجب الفعل عنده كما قاله بعض أصحابه ( كإمام الحرمين ( وفي كونه مانعا من حكم العقل ) بالحسن والقبح ( عند الخصم ) . فإذا كان داعية إلى الاختيار الموجب للفعل من فعل الله فقد تم مطلوبنا ( الثاني : ( من الوجهين وإنما ينتهض حجة على غير الجبائي ( لو كان قبح الكذب ذاتياً أي لذاته أو لصفة لازمة لذاته (لما تخلف) أن الله يوجد
Attributes of voluntary acts, for the movement of the trembling, sleeping, and unconscious one is not described in the Law as good (ḥasan) or evil (qabīḥ). It also necessitates that all obligations (takālīf) are an obligation of what cannot be borne, and no one says that. Also, the preponderant (murjīḥ) upon which the act of the servant depends is an incentive (dāʿ) necessitating his choice (ikhtiyār) which necessitates the act, and that does not negate choice but rather affirms it. This question is the solution, and what precedes it is either a refutation (naqḍ) or in its ruling. We said: As for the first: because the necessary (ḍarūrī) is the existence of power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār), not the occurrence of the act by his power and choice, and our proof is only for negating the second, not the first, so it does not contradict the necessary. As for the second, which is the refutation by the acts of the Creator, the premise that the occurring act is without a preponderant by chance (ittifāqī) is not voluntary (ikhtiyārī) is only a binding (ilzāmī) premise relative to the Muʿtazila who say that the power of the servant does not affect an act unless a preponderant, which they call the incentive (dāʿī), is joined to it; and we do not say it, for preponderance (tarjīḥ) by mere choice related to one of the two sides of the act without an incentive is permissible in our view, and that act does not exit from being voluntary (ikhtiyārī), as previously in the issue of the one fleeing from a beast and the thirsty person who finds two equal cups. If we do not say this premise, the refutation by the act of God does not apply to us. Also, on the assumption of the validity of this premise in our view, this same proof does not apply to His act, for we choose that He is capable of refraining and that His act depends on a preponderant, but that preponderant is eternal (qadīm) and does not need another preponderant such that an infinite regress (tasalsul) of preponderants would occur, as in the act of the servant if his preponderant proceeds from him, for it must be that what proceeds from him is originated (ḥādith) needing another; so the premise that if the preponderant of the act proceeds from its agent then an infinite regress is necessary is not true in His case but in the case of the servant. And to what we established, he alluded with his saying: 'So the preponderant of His agency is eternal, which is His will (irāda) and power (qudra), both based on His essence as necessitation (ījāb) and related to the act at a specific time.' So it would be a matter of chance (ittifāqī) as previously regarding the servant. I say: We may choose necessity (wujūb), and there is no harm, because the necessitating preponderant is His will based on His essence, unlike the will of the servant, for it is based on other than that; if they are necessitating, then coercion (jabr) necessarily follows. This was mentioned before with an indication of the admixture of necessitation (shāʾiba al-ījāb) in it. So that eternal preponderant does not need another preponderant such that infinite regress occurs, because the cause for needing an effective cause (muʾaththir) in our view is origination (ḥudūth), not possibility (imkān), unlike the preponderant of the servant's agency, for it is originated needing an effective cause; if its effective cause is the servant, then infinite regress occurs, and if it is another, then he is compelled (majbūr) in his act. As for the third, which is the refutation by legal (sharʿī) good and evil: It is not necessary in our view in the legally obligatory (wājib sharʿī) that the power of the agent have effect in it, rather the act must be what is ordinarily possible (maqdūr ʿāda), i.e., something accompanied by power and choice in general, and that is not sufficient in the intellectually obligatory (wājib ʿaqlī) in your view, for it requires the effect of power, so the refutation by the legal does not apply to us. As for the fourth, which is the solution: Our purpose from our proof that the servant is compelled (majbūr) and constrained (muḍṭarr) is that the servant is not independent in bringing about his act without an incentive and choice that follows that incentive and necessitates the act. That incentive along with what follows it occurs to him by the creation (khalq) of God, and we have shown his independence in this sense, and that is sufficient for the non-judgment of good and evil intellectually, for there is no difference between the act in the servant as the Shaykh said and between one who finds what necessitates the act as some of his companions, like Imām al-Ḥaramayn, said. And in its being a preventer of the intellect's judgment of good and evil according to the opponent. So if the incentive to the choice that necessitates the act is from the act of God, then our second goal from the two aspects has been accomplished, and it only applies as a proof against other than al-Jubbāʾī. If the evil (qubḥ) of lying were intrinsic (dhātī), i.e., due to its essence or a necessary attribute of its essence, then it would not be absent (lā yatakhallafu) that God causes it to exist.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive refutation of four objections raised against the Ashʿarī argument for determinism (jabr) in human acts, centered on the necessity of a preponderant cause (murjīḥ). The argument begins by asserting that voluntary acts require a preponderant cause (an incentive or dāʿī) which necessitates choice and thus the act; this does not negate choice but affirms it. The author then states that this very question is the solution, and prior objections are either refutations (naqḍ) or fall under the same ruling. The first objection claims that the argument contradicts the necessary knowledge that humans have power and choice. The author replies that what is necessarily known is the existence of power and choice, not that the act occurs by the servant's power and choice independently. Their proof only negates the latter, not the former, so no contradiction occurs. The second objection uses the acts of God as a counterexample: if divine acts also require a preponderant, they would be voluntary but not contingent. The author responds that the premise that an act without a preponderant is due to chance is a binding (ilzāmī) premise only against the Muʿtazila, who hold that power requires an incentive. Ashʿarīs reject this; they accept preponderance by mere choice without an incentive. Hence, the objection does not apply. Moreover, even accepting the premise, it does not apply to God because His preponderant (His eternal will and power) is eternal and needs no further preponderant, avoiding infinite regress. In contrast, the servant's preponderant is originated and requires a cause, leading to regress or coercion. The third objection concerns legal (sharʿī) good and evil: why are legal obligations not impossible due to lack of power? The author clarifies that in legal obligations, it is not required that power have effect; it suffices that the act is ordinarily possible, accompanied by power and choice in general. Intellectual obligations require effective power, but that is not at issue here. The fourth objection addresses the 'solution' itself: the argument that the servant is not independent of God's creation of the incentive. The author states that this suffices to negate rational judgment of good and evil, because the act's occurrence depends entirely on God, and there is no distinction between the servant's act and one who finds what necessitates the act. Thus, the second goal is achieved. Finally, the proof only applies against those who deny intrinsic goodness and evil (like al-Jubbāʾī's opponents); if evil were intrinsic, lying would never occur—but God creates it, showing that moral judgments are not intrinsic.

داع
اختيار
جبر
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming a necessary preponderant for human acts eliminates human choice entirely.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the preponderant affirms choice, as it necessitates choice and act; choice remains intact.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what does the argument for divine coercion supposedly contradict?
A) The existence of free will
B) The necessary knowledge that humans have power and choice
C) The concept of good and evil
D) The possibility of legal obligations

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī proof does not deny human power and choice, only the servant's independence in bringing about the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every choice you make is ultimately determined by an incentive created by Allah, does that make you feel trapped or truly free? Examine your heart: do you secretly resent that your independence is limited? How will you surrender that resentment to Him today?
?
The lesson teaches that your power and choice exist, but your act does not occur by them alone. When you succeed, do you credit yourself as if you alone brought it about? What would it mean for your humility and gratitude to acknowledge that every good act is ultimately from Him?
?
You have likely heard the objection that divine sovereignty eliminates human responsibility. But here, the proof shows that the preponderant affirms your choice. So ask yourself: are you using your choice to obey or to rebel? Knowing that Allah creates the incentive behind every decision, what will you do to align your incentives with His pleasure right now?

Refutation of the Second Objection: Acts of God
Refutation of the second objection regarding divine acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that divine acts constitute a counterexample does not apply to the Ashʿarī position, due to the nature of God's preponderant.

Core Idea
God's eternal preponderant avoids regress; the servant's originated preponderant requires a cause.

The second objection argues that if divine acts also require a preponderant, they would be subject to the same problem. The author responds by distinguishing between the preponderant in God, which is eternal and needs no further cause, and that in the servant, which is originated and leads to regress or coercion. This lesson examines the nuanced reply, including the premises about the Muʿtazila and the concept of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector uses the acts of God as a counterexample: if a preponderant makes an act necessary, then God's acts would be necessary and not voluntary.
2
Examine the first reply
The author first notes that the premise is only binding against the Muʿtazila, not Ashʿarīs, who allow preponderance by mere choice without an incentive.
3
Analyze the second reply
Even accepting the premise, the proof does not apply to God because His preponderant is eternal, avoiding regress. Study the explanation about regress and the difference between originated and eternal preponderants.

Attributes of voluntary acts, for the movement of the trembling, sleeping, and unconscious one is not described in the Law as good (ḥasan) or evil (qabīḥ). It also necessitates that all obligations (takālīf) are an obligation of what cannot be borne, and no one says that. Also, the preponderant (murjīḥ) upon which the act of the servant depends is an incentive (dāʿ) necessitating his choice (ikhtiyār) which necessitates the act, and that does not negate choice but rather affirms it. This question is the solution, and what precedes it is either a refutation (naqḍ) or in its ruling. We said: As for the first: because the necessary (ḍarūrī) is the existence of power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār), not the occurrence of the act by his power and choice, and our proof is only for negating the second, not the first, so it does not contradict the necessary. As for the second, which is the refutation by the acts of the Creator, the premise that the occurring act is without a preponderant by chance (ittifāqī) is not voluntary (ikhtiyārī) is only a binding (ilzāmī) premise relative to the Muʿtazila who say that the power of the servant does not affect an act unless a preponderant, which they call the incentive (dāʿī), is joined to it; and we do not say it, for preponderance (tarjīḥ) by mere choice related to one of the two sides of the act without an incentive is permissible in our view, and that act does not exit from being voluntary (ikhtiyārī), as previously in the issue of the one fleeing from a beast and the thirsty person who finds two equal cups. If we do not say this premise, the refutation by the act of God does not apply to us. Also, on the assumption of the validity of this premise in our view, this same proof does not apply to His act, for we choose that He is capable of refraining and that His act depends on a preponderant, but that preponderant is eternal (qadīm) and does not need another preponderant such that an infinite regress (tasalsul) of preponderants would occur, as in the act of the servant if his preponderant proceeds from him, for it must be that what proceeds from him is originated (ḥādith) needing another; so the premise that if the preponderant of the act proceeds from its agent then an infinite regress is necessary is not true in His case but in the case of the servant. And to what we established, he alluded with his saying: 'So the preponderant of His agency is eternal, which is His will (irāda) and power (qudra), both based on His essence as necessitation (ījāb) and related to the act at a specific time.' So it would be a matter of chance (ittifāqī) as previously regarding the servant. I say: We may choose necessity (wujūb), and there is no harm, because the necessitating preponderant is His will based on His essence, unlike the will of the servant, for it is based on other than that; if they are necessitating, then coercion (jabr) necessarily follows. This was mentioned before with an indication of the admixture of necessitation (shāʾiba al-ījāb) in it. So that eternal preponderant does not need another preponderant such that infinite regress occurs, because the cause for needing an effective cause (muʾaththir) in our view is origination (ḥudūth), not possibility (imkān), unlike the preponderant of the servant's agency, for it is originated needing an effective cause; if its effective cause is the servant, then infinite regress occurs, and if it is another, then he is compelled (majbūr) in his act. As for the third, which is the refutation by legal (sharʿī) good and evil: It is not necessary in our view in the legally obligatory (wājib sharʿī) that the power of the agent have effect in it, rather the act must be what is ordinarily possible (maqdūr ʿāda), i.e., something accompanied by power and choice in general, and that is not sufficient in the intellectually obligatory (wājib ʿaqlī) in your view, for it requires the effect of power, so the refutation by the legal does not apply to us. As for the fourth, which is the solution: Our purpose from our proof that the servant is compelled (majbūr) and constrained (muḍṭarr) is that the servant is not independent in bringing about his act without an incentive and choice that follows that incentive and necessitates the act. That incentive along with what follows it occurs to him by the creation (khalq) of God, and we have shown his independence in this sense, and that is sufficient for the non-judgment of good and evil intellectually, for there is no difference between the act in the servant as the Shaykh said and between one who finds what necessitates the act as some of his companions, like Imām al-Ḥaramayn, said. And in its being a preventer of the intellect's judgment of good and evil according to the opponent. So if the incentive to the choice that necessitates the act is from the act of God, then our second goal from the two aspects has been accomplished, and it only applies as a proof against other than al-Jubbāʾī. If the evil (qubḥ) of lying were intrinsic (dhātī), i.e., due to its essence or a necessary attribute of its essence, then it would not be absent (lā yatakhallafu) that God causes it to exist.

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive refutation of four objections raised against the Ashʿarī argument for determinism (jabr) in human acts, centered on the necessity of a preponderant cause (murjīḥ). The argument begins by asserting that voluntary acts require a preponderant cause (an incentive or dāʿī) which necessitates choice and thus the act; this does not negate choice but affirms it. The author then states that this very question is the solution, and prior objections are either refutations (naqḍ) or fall under the same ruling. The first objection claims that the argument contradicts the necessary knowledge that humans have power and choice. The author replies that what is necessarily known is the existence of power and choice, not that the act occurs by the servant's power and choice independently. Their proof only negates the latter, not the former, so no contradiction occurs. The second objection uses the acts of God as a counterexample: if divine acts also require a preponderant, they would be voluntary but not contingent. The author responds that the premise that an act without a preponderant is due to chance is a binding (ilzāmī) premise only against the Muʿtazila, who hold that power requires an incentive. Ashʿarīs reject this; they accept preponderance by mere choice without an incentive. Hence, the objection does not apply. Moreover, even accepting the premise, it does not apply to God because His preponderant (His eternal will and power) is eternal and needs no further preponderant, avoiding infinite regress. In contrast, the servant's preponderant is originated and requires a cause, leading to regress or coercion. The third objection concerns legal (sharʿī) good and evil: why are legal obligations not impossible due to lack of power? The author clarifies that in legal obligations, it is not required that power have effect; it suffices that the act is ordinarily possible, accompanied by power and choice in general. Intellectual obligations require effective power, but that is not at issue here. The fourth objection addresses the 'solution' itself: the argument that the servant is not independent of God's creation of the incentive. The author states that this suffices to negate rational judgment of good and evil, because the act's occurrence depends entirely on God, and there is no distinction between the servant's act and one who finds what necessitates the act. Thus, the second goal is achieved. Finally, the proof only applies against those who deny intrinsic goodness and evil (like al-Jubbāʾī's opponents); if evil were intrinsic, lying would never occur—but God creates it, showing that moral judgments are not intrinsic.

Why does the second objection not apply according to the first part of the reply?
A) Because God's acts need no preponderant

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you cannot find a reason for why something happens, do you trust that God's eternal wisdom holds a preponderant beyond your understanding? Or do you let doubt creep in? What is one area of your life where you are demanding a reason from God, rather than submitting to His eternal will, and how will you actively surrender that to Him today?
?
The passage distinguishes between God's self-based will and the servant's dependent will. How often do you act out of a desire to be independent, even though your very will is originated and dependent on God? Can you identify a recent decision where you pretended to be self-sufficient, forgetting that your own preponderant is not eternal and needs God's support?
?
If the servant's act, when caused by anything other than himself, leads to coercion (jabr), then consider: In your daily actions, are you truly acting as a free agent, or are you being compelled by desires, habits, or social pressures? How will you exercise your power this week to consciously choose obedience to God over compulsion from within or without?

Refutation of the Third and Fourth Objections: Legal Good/Evil and the Solution
Refutation of the third and fourth objections and conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ashʿarī argument handles legal obligations and why the servant's lack of independence suffices to negate rational moral judgment.

Core Idea
Legal obligations require ordinary possibility, not effective power; the servant's dependence on God negates rational good and evil.

The third objection concerns legal good and evil: if the servant lacks effective power, legal obligations would be impossible. The author clarifies that in legal obligations, it suffices that the act is ordinarily possible, i.e., accompanied by power and choice in general, not that power has effect. The fourth objection is the 'solution' itself: the claim that the servant is not independent. The author argues that this is sufficient to negate rational judgment of good and evil, because the act's occurrence depends entirely on God. The lesson also touches on the implication for intrinsic evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection and reply
Understand the claim that legal obligations would be impossible if power lacks effect. Note the distinction between legally obligatory (wājib sharʿī) and intellectually obligatory (wājib ʿaqlī).
2
Examine the fourth objection and reply
The so-called 'solution' is that the servant's incentive is created by God, making him not independent. See why this suffices to negate rational moral judgment.
3
Reflect on the concluding remark
The author notes that if evil were intrinsic, God would not create lying, but He does, so moral judgments are not intrinsic.

( وأما الثالث : ( وهو النقض بالحسن والقبح الشرعيين ( فلا يجب عندنا في الواجب الشرعي تأثير قدرة الفاعل فيه بل يجب أن يكون الفعل مما هو مقدور عادة) أي يكون مما يقارنه القدرة والاختيار في الجملة ولا يكفي ذلك في الواجب العقلي عندكم إذ لا بد فيه من تأثير القدرة فلا يتجه علينا النقض بالشرعي. ( وأما الرابع : ) وهو الحل ( فمقصودنا ) من دليلنا على كون العبد مجبوراً مضطراً ) أن العبد غير مستقل بإيجاد فعله من غير داع واختيار يترتب على ذلك الداعي ويوجب الفعل ( يحصل ) أي ذلك الداعي مع ما يترتب عليه ) له بخلق الله تعالى إياه، وقد بيناه ) عدم استقلاله بهذا المعنى ) وذلك كاف في عدم الحكم ) بالحسن و القبح ( عقلاً إذ لا فرق بين (الفعل) في العبد كما قاله الشيخ، وبين يوجد ما يجب الفعل عنده كما قاله بعض أصحابه ( كإمام الحرمين ( وفي كونه مانعا من حكم العقل ) بالحسن والقبح ( عند الخصم ) . فإذا كان داعية إلى الاختيار الموجب للفعل من فعل الله فقد تم مطلوبنا ( الثاني : ( من الوجهين وإنما ينتهض حجة على غير الجبائي ( لو كان قبح الكذب ذاتياً أي لذاته أو لصفة لازمة لذاته (لما تخلف) أن الله يوجد
Attributes of voluntary acts, for the movement of the trembling, sleeping, and unconscious one is not described in the Law as good (ḥasan) or evil (qabīḥ). It also necessitates that all obligations (takālīf) are an obligation of what cannot be borne, and no one says that. Also, the preponderant (murjīḥ) upon which the act of the servant depends is an incentive (dāʿ) necessitating his choice (ikhtiyār) which necessitates the act, and that does not negate choice but rather affirms it. This question is the solution, and what precedes it is either a refutation (naqḍ) or in its ruling. We said: As for the first: because the necessary (ḍarūrī) is the existence of power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār), not the occurrence of the act by his power and choice, and our proof is only for negating the second, not the first, so it does not contradict the necessary. As for the second, which is the refutation by the acts of the Creator, the premise that the occurring act is without a preponderant by chance (ittifāqī) is not voluntary (ikhtiyārī) is only a binding (ilzāmī) premise relative to the Muʿtazila who say that the power of the servant does not affect an act unless a preponderant, which they call the incentive (dāʿī), is joined to it; and we do not say it, for preponderance (tarjīḥ) by mere choice related to one of the two sides of the act without an incentive is permissible in our view, and that act does not exit from being voluntary (ikhtiyārī), as previously in the issue of the one fleeing from a beast and the thirsty person who finds two equal cups. If we do not say this premise, the refutation by the act of God does not apply to us. Also, on the assumption of the validity of this premise in our view, this same proof does not apply to His act, for we choose that He is capable of refraining and that His act depends on a preponderant, but that preponderant is eternal (qadīm) and does not need another preponderant such that an infinite regress (tasalsul) of preponderants would occur, as in the act of the servant if his preponderant proceeds from him, for it must be that what proceeds from him is originated (ḥādith) needing another; so the premise that if the preponderant of the act proceeds from its agent then an infinite regress is necessary is not true in His case but in the case of the servant. And to what we established, he alluded with his saying: 'So the preponderant of His agency is eternal, which is His will (irāda) and power (qudra), both based on His essence as necessitation (ījāb) and related to the act at a specific time.' So it would be a matter of chance (ittifāqī) as previously regarding the servant. I say: We may choose necessity (wujūb), and there is no harm, because the necessitating preponderant is His will based on His essence, unlike the will of the servant, for it is based on other than that; if they are necessitating, then coercion (jabr) necessarily follows. This was mentioned before with an indication of the admixture of necessitation (shāʾiba al-ījāb) in it. So that eternal preponderant does not need another preponderant such that infinite regress occurs, because the cause for needing an effective cause (muʾaththir) in our view is origination (ḥudūth), not possibility (imkān), unlike the preponderant of the servant's agency, for it is originated needing an effective cause; if its effective cause is the servant, then infinite regress occurs, and if it is another, then he is compelled (majbūr) in his act. As for the third, which is the refutation by legal (sharʿī) good and evil: It is not necessary in our view in the legally obligatory (wājib sharʿī) that the power of the agent have effect in it, rather the act must be what is ordinarily possible (maqdūr ʿāda), i.e., something accompanied by power and choice in general, and that is not sufficient in the intellectually obligatory (wājib ʿaqlī) in your view, for it requires the effect of power, so the refutation by the legal does not apply to us. As for the fourth, which is the solution: Our purpose from our proof that the servant is compelled (majbūr) and constrained (muḍṭarr) is that the servant is not independent in bringing about his act without an incentive and choice that follows that incentive and necessitates the act. That incentive along with what follows it occurs to him by the creation (khalq) of God, and we have shown his independence in this sense, and that is sufficient for the non-judgment of good and evil intellectually, for there is no difference between the act in the servant as the Shaykh said and between one who finds what necessitates the act as some of his companions, like Imām al-Ḥaramayn, said. And in its being a preventer of the intellect's judgment of good and evil according to the opponent. So if the incentive to the choice that necessitates the act is from the act of God, then our second goal from the two aspects has been accomplished, and it only applies as a proof against other than al-Jubbāʾī. If the evil (qubḥ) of lying were intrinsic (dhātī), i.e., due to its essence or a necessary attribute of its essence, then it would not be absent (lā yatakhallafu) that God causes it to exist.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive refutation of four objections raised against the Ashʿarī argument for determinism (jabr) in human acts, centered on the necessity of a preponderant cause (murjīḥ). The argument begins by asserting that voluntary acts require a preponderant cause (an incentive or dāʿī) which necessitates choice and thus the act; this does not negate choice but affirms it. The author then states that this very question is the solution, and prior objections are either refutations (naqḍ) or fall under the same ruling. The first objection claims that the argument contradicts the necessary knowledge that humans have power and choice. The author replies that what is necessarily known is the existence of power and choice, not that the act occurs by the servant's power and choice independently. Their proof only negates the latter, not the former, so no contradiction occurs. The second objection uses the acts of God as a counterexample: if divine acts also require a preponderant, they would be voluntary but not contingent. The author responds that the premise that an act without a preponderant is due to chance is a binding (ilzāmī) premise only against the Muʿtazila, who hold that power requires an incentive. Ashʿarīs reject this; they accept preponderance by mere choice without an incentive. Hence, the objection does not apply. Moreover, even accepting the premise, it does not apply to God because His preponderant (His eternal will and power) is eternal and needs no further preponderant, avoiding infinite regress. In contrast, the servant's preponderant is originated and requires a cause, leading to regress or coercion. The third objection concerns legal (sharʿī) good and evil: why are legal obligations not impossible due to lack of power? The author clarifies that in legal obligations, it is not required that power have effect; it suffices that the act is ordinarily possible, accompanied by power and choice in general. Intellectual obligations require effective power, but that is not at issue here. The fourth objection addresses the 'solution' itself: the argument that the servant is not independent of God's creation of the incentive. The author states that this suffices to negate rational judgment of good and evil, because the act's occurrence depends entirely on God, and there is no distinction between the servant's act and one who finds what necessitates the act. Thus, the second goal is achieved. Finally, the proof only applies against those who deny intrinsic goodness and evil (like al-Jubbāʾī's opponents); if evil were intrinsic, lying would never occur—but God creates it, showing that moral judgments are not intrinsic.

مقدور عادة
واجب شرعي
واجب عقلي
جبر
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the servant is not independent, then legal obligations are impossible.
Correction: The author shows that legal obligations only require ordinary possibility (accompanied by power and choice), not effective power.

Checkpoint
According to the reply to the third objection, what is required for a legally obligatory act?
A) That the power of the agent has effect
B) That the act is ordinarily possible and accompanied by power and choice
C) That the act is intrinsically good
D) That the agent is independent

Key Takeaway
The servant's lack of independence due to God's creation of the incentive is sufficient to negate rational judgment of good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every incentive that leads you to act is created by Allah, then where is the room for you to claim credit for any good deed? How does this strip away your self-righteousness and force you to rely entirely on Him?
?
When you find yourself hesitating between right and wrong, do you truly believe that even the desire to do good is a gift from Allah? In what specific area of your life are you still acting as if you are the independent source of your choices?
?
Consider a moment when you felt proud of a moral decision you made. According to this lesson, your pride is misplaced because the entire chain of incentive and choice was from Allah. Can you honestly let go of that pride today and attribute every good in your life to Him alone? Commit to a specific action that reminds you of this dependence.

Two Arguments That Allah's Actions Are Not Caused by Purposes
Two arguments against purposes in Allah's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two logical arguments from classical kalam that show Allah's actions cannot be caused by purposes: the first based on arbitrariness of one-way dependency, and the second based on infinite regress.

Core Idea
Allah's actions are free from purpose; otherwise logic leads to arbitrariness or infinite regress.

This passage presents two powerful arguments from the classical kalam tradition against the idea that Allah's actions are motivated by purposes. The first argument shows that if some actions were purposes for others without reciprocity, that would be arbitrary. The second argument demonstrates that any chain of purposes must terminate in an action that is intended for itself, which then has no purpose, thereby undermining the necessity of purpose altogether. Together, they defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full literal translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the parenthetical 'And also' that introduces the second argument.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument says: if some actions were purposes for others without the reverse (one-way dependency), that is pure arbitrariness (تحكم بحت). Ask yourself why this is arbitrary and what it implies for causation in Allah's actions.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument says: if every action has a purpose, then purposes regress infinitely unless we end at an action intended in itself. But that intrinsic purpose has no purpose, which contradicts the original assumption that all actions have purposes. Trace this logic carefully.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that the very logic of purpose leads to either arbitrariness or an infinite regress, both impossible for Allah. Therefore, His actions are not caused by purposes.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why this doctrine matters: it protects the idea that Allah is not subject to external or internal motivations, preserving His absolute freedom and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : أفعال الله تعالى ليست معللة بالأغراض بعضها غرضاً من بعض آخر دون عكسه تحكم بحت فلا يتصور تعليل في أفعاله أصلاً ( وأيضاً ) إذا عللت أفعاله بالأغراض فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما هو الغرض) والمقصود في نفسه وإلا تسلسلت الأغراض إلى ما لا نهاية لها ( ولا يكون ذلك ) الذي هو غرض ومقصود في نفسه ( لغرض آخر لأنه خلاف ما فرض ( وإذا جاز ذلك بطل القول بوجوب الغرض ( إذ قد انتهى أفعاله إلى فعل لا غرض له وهو الذي كان مقصوداً في نفسه
Sixth Observatory - Eighth Aim: The actions of Allah, exalted, are not caused by purposes. Some of them being a purpose for another without the reverse is pure arbitrariness, so no causation is conceivable in His actions at all. (And also) if His actions are caused by purposes, then it is necessary to end at what is the purpose and intended in itself, otherwise the purposes would regress infinitely. And that which is a purpose and intended in itself cannot be for another purpose because that contradicts what was assumed. And if that is permissible, the statement of the necessity of purpose is invalidated, because His actions would end at an action that has no purpose, which is what was intended in itself.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the matn (original text) against the attribution of purposes (أغراض) to Allah's actions. The first argument: if some actions were purposes for others without the reverse (i.e., a one-way dependency), that would be pure arbitrariness (تحكم بحت), so no purposive causation is conceivable in His actions at all. The second argument: if every action had a purpose, then that purpose would itself be an action or result, which would require another purpose, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). Therefore, we must end at an action that is intended in itself, which then has no purpose, thus invalidating the necessity of purpose. The two arguments together show that the very logic of purpose leads to either arbitrariness or an infinite chain, both of which are impossible for Allah.

Key Terms
أغراض
تحكم
تسلسل
غرض مقصود في نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the infinite regress argument only applies to external purposes, but it also applies to any sequential dependency.
Correction: The argument applies to any chain of purposes, whether external or internal, because the logic of regress is independent of the nature of the purposes.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what is the consequence of some actions being purposes for others without the reverse?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It is pure arbitrariness.
C) It implies a first purpose.
D) It proves the necessity of purpose.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions are not caused by purposes; any attempt to attribute purposes leads to either arbitrariness or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty in your life, do you instinctively demand to know Allah's 'purpose' for it? How does this lesson challenge your need for a reason behind every trial? Will you now strive to submit without requiring an explanation?
?
This lesson argues that Allah's actions are not caused by purposes—yet you constantly pray for specific outcomes. Does your dua reflect a desire for Allah to act for your purposes, or are you truly submitting to His will without expecting a reason? How would your prayer change if you internalized this teaching?
?
Consider a time you judged an event in your life as 'meaningless' or 'unfair.' Were you implicitly assuming that Allah's actions must serve a purpose you can understand? How can you cultivate trust in Allah's wisdom without needing to know the purpose behind His decree?

The Objection of Conceptual Distinction and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation on purpose distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conceptual distinction alone is insufficient to avoid circularity in divine purpose and how the refutation preserves the necessity of real distinction.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction still leads to ontological circularity; real distinction is necessary.

In kalam discussions of divine purpose, an objection arises: perhaps the purpose need only be conceptually distinct from the agent, not separate in essence. This lesson presents that objection and its sharp refutation. By analyzing the logical implications, you'll see why a mere conceptual distinction fails to avoid the problem of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objection is introduced: 'وقد يقال : لا يجب في الغرض كونه مغايرا بالذات، بل يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري'. Understand the claim that conceptual distinction suffices.
The objection is based on notes from al-Mutala'.
2
Identify the basis of the objection
The commentator explains that this objection relies on the idea that a thing can be its own goal if its mental existence causes its external existence. Notice the terminology: 'غاية لنفسه', 'وجود ذهني', 'وجود خارجي'.
3
Analyze the refutation
Read the refutation: 'وفيه نظر إذ يلزم منه أن يكون غاية الشيء معلولاً له بحسب الخارج ومترتباً عليه فلايتصور بينهما الاتحاد بالذات'. See how the refutation shows that conceptual distinction leads to the purpose being an effect of the action externally.
4
Connect to broader doctrine
Consider why this matters for divine attributes and action: if Allah's purpose were not really distinct from His essence, it would entail that His action produces its own goal, which is impossible.
This reinforces that Allah's purpose must be a real separate attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لا يجب في الغرض كونه مغايرا بالذات، بل يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري قوله : ( بل يكفيه التغاير (الاعتباري هذا بناء على ما ذكره في حواشي المطالع من أنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء غاية لنفسه إلا أن يكون وجوده الذهني علة لوجوده الخارجي لا محذور فيه، وفيه نظر إذ يلزم منه أن يكون غاية الشيء معلولاً له بحسب الخارج ومترتباً عليه فلايتصور بينهما الاتحاد بالذات
It may be said: It is not necessary in the purpose that it be distinct in essence, rather conceptual distinction suffices. [Commentator's note:] His saying: 'rather conceptual distinction suffices' is based on what was mentioned in the marginalia of al-Mutala' that the thing being a goal for itself does not necessitate anything except that its mental existence is a cause for its external existence, which contains no problem. But there is a consideration in it because it would necessitate that the goal of a thing be a resultant effect of it externally and dependent upon it, so unity in essence between them is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its refutation. The objection (وقد يقال) is that for something to be a purpose, it does not have to be a separate entity; it is enough that there is a conceptual distinction (تغاير اعتباري) between the agent and the purpose within the same action. The commentator (the sharh) then addresses this by quoting the phrase 'بل يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري' and explaining that this is based on the idea from حواشي المطالع (marginalia of al-Mutala') that if something is its own goal, its mental existence can be the cause of its external existence—a notion that is not problematic in itself. However, the commentator finds a flaw: if the goal is a mental cause of the external action, then externally the goal would be an effect of the action and dependent on it, making it impossible for the action and its goal to be united in essence (اتحاد بالذات). So the objection fails because conceptual distinction alone does not avoid the problem of circularity or regress.

Key Terms
تغاير اعتباري
غاية لنفسه
وجود ذهني
وجود خارجي
اتحاد بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think conceptual distinction suffices to avoid the problem of circularity in divine purpose.
Correction: Conceptual distinction still leads to the goal being an effect of the action externally, which is impossible for Allah's attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator identify as problematic about the objection that conceptual distinction suffices for a purpose?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of purposes.
B) It implies that the goal is an effect of the action externally, making unity in essence inconceivable.
C) It denies that Allah acts with purpose.
D) It contradicts the apparent meaning of revelation.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual distinction between agent and purpose is insufficient because it implies the purpose is an effect of the action, precluding essential unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you ever confuse the mental intention of ‘seeking Allah’s pleasure’ with the actual outcome of the action? How can you ensure your purpose is truly distinct and not just a conceptual label you attach to your own desires?
?
This lesson shows that mental distinctions do not resolve real contradictions. Where in your life are you content with conceptual sincerity while your actions reveal a different reality? What step can you take today to align them?
?
The commentator warns that a goal that is merely conceptually distinct still entails dependence and circularity. In your relationship with Allah, are you seeking Him as a real goal outside yourself, or are you using religious concepts to serve your own self-image? What evidence do you have?

The Mu'tazilite Argument from Purpose and Its Refutation
Mu'tazilite proof and Ash'ari refutation by term analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite proof that Allah's actions must have a purpose, and the Ash'ari dialectical refutation that shifts the burden of proof by demanding a clear definition of 'عبث'.

Core Idea
The burden of proof shifts when the opponent's key term is undefined.

The Mu'tazila argued that any action without purpose is vain and evil, so Allah, being wise, must act for a purpose benefiting others. The response does not simply reject this but examines the term 'عبث' and shows that if it means 'no purpose', it is the very point of dispute; if it means something else, the Mu'tazila must define it, prove it applies, and prove it is impossible for Allah. This passage illustrates a classic kalam strategy of challenging definitions to refute an argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysing. Let the structure of argument and response come through.
Notice how the response is structured as a conditional analysis.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'عبث', 'غرض', 'قبيح', and the three steps: تصوير, تقرير, دلالة على امتناع.
These terms are central to the dialectical move.
3
Reflect on the dialectical strategy
Ask yourself: How does the response shift the burden of proof? Why does it start by clarifying the meaning of 'عبث'?
This shows the importance of defining terms before arguing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أي المعتزلة على وجود الغرض في أفعاله تعالى ( بأن الفعل الخالي عن الغرض عبث وأنه قبيح) بالضرورة ( يجب تنزيه الله عنه ) لكونه عالماً بقبحه واستغنائه عنه فلا بد إذن في فعله من غرض يعود إلى غيره نفيا للعبث والنقض (قلنا) في جوابهم : (إن أردتم بالعبث ما لاغرض فيه ) من الأفعال ( فهو أول المسألة المتنازع فيها إذ نحن نجوز أن يصدر عنه تعالى فعل لا غرض فيه أصلا وأنتم تمنعونه وتعبرون عنه بالعبث فلا يجديكم نفعاً ، ( وإن أرتم ) بالعبث ( أمراً آخر فلا بد ) لكم أولاً : ( من تصويره)، أي تصوير ذلك الأمر الآخر حتى نفهمه ونتصوره ( ثم) لا بد ثانياً : ( من تقريره ) أي بيانه ثبوت ذلك المفهوم للفعل على تقدير خلوه من الغرض، ( ثم) لا بد ثالثاً : ( من الدلالة على امتناعه ) أي استحالة الفعل المتصف بذلك المفهوم الآخر ) على الله سبحانه وتعالى ( حتى يتم لكم مطلوبكم
The Mu'tazila argued for the existence of purpose in His actions (by saying that an action devoid of purpose is vain and that it is evil) by necessity (and it is necessary to declare Allah free from it) because He knows its evil and is independent of it. Therefore, there must be a purpose in His action that returns to other than Him, negating vanity and disproval. (We said) in answer to them: (If you mean by 'vain' that which has no purpose) among actions, then that is precisely the matter of dispute, for we allow that an action with no purpose at all may issue from Him, and you forbid it and call it 'vain', so this does not benefit you. (And if you mean) by 'vain' (something else), then it is necessary (for you first: to depict it), i.e., to depict that other thing so that we may understand and conceive it; (then) it is necessary second: (to establish it), i.e., to demonstrate that concept applies to the action on the assumption that it is devoid of purpose; (then) it is necessary third: (to prove its impossibility), i.e., the impossibility of the action characterized by that other concept from Allah, exalted, so that your claim may be complete.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage records the Mu'tazilite proof and the response. The Mu'tazila (المعتزلة) argue that any action without a purpose (غرض) is vain (عبث) and ugly (قبيح) by necessity, and Allah, being wise and independent, must be free from such actions. Therefore, every action of Allah must have a purpose that benefits someone other than Himself. The answer (قلنا) takes a dialectical approach. First, the term 'عبث' is examined. If by 'عبث' you mean an action with no purpose at all, then this is exactly the point of disagreement: we (the Ash'aris) affirm that Allah can do such actions, while you deny it, so calling it 'عبث' adds no force. If you mean something else, then three conditions must be met: (1) define that something clearly; (2) establish that an action devoid of purpose actually has that negative attribute; (3) prove that such an attribute is impossible for Allah. Until these three are fulfilled, the argument is not proven. This refutation shifts the burden of proof onto the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
عبث
قبيح
غرض
تصوير
تقرير
دلالة على امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari response simply denies that Allah's actions are vain.
Correction: Actually, the response challenges the definition of 'عبث' and demands proof, shifting the burden of proof rather than merely denying the claim.

Checkpoint
What is the first condition the response demands if the Mu'tazila mean something other than 'no purpose' by 'عبث'?
A) To prove the action is impossible for Allah.
B) To depict that other meaning clearly.
C) To establish that the action has that meaning.
D) To show that the action is beneficial.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite purpose argument demands a clear definition of 'عبث' and proof, shifting the burden of proof onto the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems utterly purposeless, does your heart immediately rebel, thinking 'this is vain'? The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot do vain actions. But what if the very concept of 'vain' is unclear? Are you willing to suspend your judgment and trust that God's wisdom may be beyond your definition of purpose?
?
The Ash'ari response demands that terms be depicted, established, and proven before they are used against God. How often do you make claims about God—such as 'He wouldn't do this'—without first examining what you mean by those terms? This lesson calls you to intellectual honesty before God. Will you commit to defining your words before judging His actions?

Divine Benefits vs. Purposes
Distinguishes between purpose and benefit in divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between purpose (gharad) and benefit (ghāya) in divine actions, and why benefits are consequences, not motivations for Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's actions are full of benefits, but those benefits are effects, not motivations.

In this passage, the author refines the concept of 'vain action' by redefining it as lacking benefits, not lacking purpose. He then clarifies that while Allah's actions are filled with wisdom and benefit for His creatures, these benefits are not the motivating causes for His acts. This preserves both divine wisdom and divine self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: عبث, فوائد, منافع, أسباب باعثة, علل غائية, غايات.
This passage is a response to a Mu'tazilite objection.
2
Identify the distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'purpose' (gharad) as a motivating cause and 'benefit' (ghāya) as a consequence. Find where this distinction is made.
The key line: 'لا تكون أغراضاً له ولا عللاً غائية... بل تكون غايات ومنافع...'
3
Understand the interpretive principle
The passage explains how to interpret scriptural verses that seem to give reasons for Allah's actions. They are to be interpreted as referring to the end and benefit, not to a purpose that implies need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في الجواب : إن العبث ما كان خالياً عن الفوائد والمنافع وأفعاله تعالى محكمة متقنة مشتملة على حكم ومصالح لا تحصى راجعة إلى مخلوقاته تعالى، لكنها ليست أسباباً باعثة على إقدامه وعللاً مقتضية لفاعليته فلا تكون أغراضاً له ولا عللاً غائية لأفعاله حتى يلزم استكماله بها، بل تكون غايات ومنافع لأفعاله وآثاراً مترتبة عليها فلا يلزم أن يكون شيء من أفعاله عبثاً خالياً عن الفوائد، وما ورد من الظواهر الدالة على تعليل أفعاله تعالى فهو محمول على الغاية والمنفعة دون الغرض والعلة الغائية
It may be said in answer: Vain is that which is devoid of benefits and advantages, and His actions are well-ordered, precise, containing wisdoms and benefits beyond count that return to His creatures. However, they are not motivating causes for His undertaking or efficient causes for His agency, so they are not purposes for Him nor final causes for His actions such that He would be perfected by them. Rather, they are ends and benefits for His actions and effects consequent upon them. Therefore, it does not follow that any of His actions is vain, devoid of benefits. And what has come in the apparent meanings (zawāhir) that indicate the causation of His actions is interpreted as referring to the end and benefit, not to the purpose and final cause.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a different answer to the Mu'tazilite claim. It redefines 'عبث' (vain) as that which is devoid of benefits (فوائد) and advantages (منافع), rather than devoid of purpose. Then it asserts that Allah's actions are full of wisdom and benefits for creatures, so they are not vain. However, these benefits are not the motivating causes (أسباب باعثة) for Allah's actions, nor are they final causes (علل غائية) that complete Him. Rather, they are merely the ends (غايات) and effects (آثار) that follow from His actions. Thus, the apparent scriptural indications that seem to give reasons for Allah's actions are interpreted as referring to the benefit and end (الغاية والمنفعة), not to a purpose (الغرض) or final cause that would imply need. This preserves both the fact that Allah's actions have wisdom and the doctrine that He is self-sufficient and not motivated by external ends.

Key Terms
عبث
فوائد
منافع
أسباب باعثة
علل غائية
غايات
ظواهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah's actions have benefits, then He must be acting for those benefits (i.e., with a purpose).
Correction: Benefits are consequences, not motivations. Allah acts without need for any purpose, but His actions have beneficial outcomes for creatures.

Checkpoint
According to this answer, how should scriptural verses that seem to give a reason for Allah's actions be interpreted?
A) As indicating that Allah acts for a purpose.
B) As referring to the benefit and end, not to a purpose or final cause.
C) As metaphorical and not real.
D) As abrogated by rational arguments.

Key Takeaway
Benefits are real outcomes of divine actions, but they do not motivate Allah; they are effects, not purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you secretly question whether Allah truly cares for you because you cannot see an immediate benefit? How will you trust His wisdom now, knowing that benefits are real but not His motivation?
?
In your acts of worship, do you ever feel that you are fulfilling a need in Allah? Confront this thought in light of the passage: what change must you make in your intention starting today?
?
You have just read that Allah’s actions contain countless benefits for you. Are you living as though you believe that—grateful and patient? If not, what step will you take today to align your heart with this truth?

Reward Is Not Proportional to Hardship
Author argues that reward is not tied to hardship, so hardships in commands are not for reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical Sunni theologians argue that reward is not tied to hardship, and how this refutes the notion that divine commands have a purpose.

Core Idea
Reward does not depend on difficulty; even a word of faith can outweigh arduous worship.

This passage tackles a key debate about divine actions: are they motivated by purposes? The author argues that if reward is not proportional to hardship, then commanding hardship cannot be for reward. This challenges a common assumption and clarifies a hadith about the best worship being the most strenuous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: the author sets up the argument, gives examples, and qualifies a hadith.
The passage is dense; focus on the main claim first.
2
Identify the core premise
Find where the author states that reward is not proportional to hardship. Look at the examples: shahada vs. prayer/fasting, and the word that saves a prophet.
The author uses 'do you not see' to highlight the examples.
3
Understand the hadith qualification
Notice how the author restricts the hadith 'best worship is the most strenuous' to cases where benefits are equal. This prevents a contradiction.
The qualification preserves the principle that reward is based on benefit.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this refutes the idea that divine commands have a purpose (gharad). If reward can be given without hardship, commanding hardship is purposeless.
This supports the Ash'ari position that God's acts are not for purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : أفعال الله تعالى ليست معللة بالأغراض الانتفاع والتضرر لا من الله تعالى فإنه يجوز أن يتفضل به ) كما تفضل ) على عباده ( بما لا يحصى من النعم في الدنيا ) . وأنت خبير بأن المستقبح عندهم هو التفضل بالتعظيم الموعود دون النعم كما صورناه لكنه سند للمنع فلا يجدي دفعه ( وإن سلم تعالى أيضاً فيمكن التعريض (له أي للثواب ( بدون هذه المشاق ) العظيمة (إذ ليس الثواب على قدر المشقة وعوضاً مساوياً لها، (ألا ترى في أن التلفظ بكلمة الشهادة من الثواب ما ليس في كثير من العبادات الشاقة ) كالصلاة والصيام ) وكذا الكلمة المتضمنة لإنجاء نبي من ظالم يريد إهلاكه (أو تمهيد قاعدة خير أو دفع شر عام)، إذ يستحق بهذه الكلمة من الثواب مايزيد على ثواب كثير من العبادات وإن كانت أشق منها . ( وما يروى ) من ( أن أفضل العبادات أحمزها ) أي أشقها ( فذلك عند التساوي في المصالح ) فلا ينافي أن يكون الأخف الأسهل أكثر ثواباً إذا كان أكثر مصلحة وأعظم فائدة، وإذا أمكن التعريض المذكور بدون تلك المشاق كان التكليف بها عاريا عن الغرض .
The Sixth Observation - Eighth Goal: The acts of God are not motivated by purposes. Benefit and harm are not from God, for He may bestow favors as He has bestowed upon His servants countless blessings in this world. You are aware that what they consider objectionable is the bestowal of the promised exaltation without [such] blessings, as we have described; but this is a support for the denial, so its refutation is of no avail. Even if it is granted, it is possible to expose [one] to reward without these great hardships, since reward is not in proportion to hardship nor an equivalent compensation for it. Do you not see that uttering the word of testimony (kalimat al-shahada) brings a reward that is not [found] in many arduous acts of worship, such as prayer and fasting? Likewise, the word that saves a prophet from an oppressor who intends to destroy him, or the establishment of a good principle or the removal of general harm, for by this word one deserves a reward that exceeds the reward of many acts of worship even if they are more arduous. And what is narrated that the best acts of worship are the most strenuous means that is when the benefits are equal; so it does not contradict that the lighter and easier [act] has more reward if it has more benefit and greater advantage. And if the aforementioned exposure [to reward] is possible without those hardships, then the command [to perform] them is devoid of purpose.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is part of a larger argument refuting the claim that divine commands are for a purpose (gharad). The author argues that if reward is not tied to hardship, then the hardships commanded in worship are not for the sake of reward. He illustrates with the example of the shahada, which is easy but immensely rewarding, more so than harder acts like prayer and fasting. He counters the hadith that the best acts are the most strenuous by qualifying that it applies only when merits are equal. Thus, if reward can be given without hardship, commanding hardship is purposeless. This is a direct challenge to the Mu'tazilite view that divine actions are for the benefit of creation.

Misconception: The hadith 'the best worship is the most strenuous' is taken absolutely, implying that harder acts always earn more reward.
Correction: The hadith applies only when the benefits are equal; otherwise, an easier act can have more reward if it has greater benefit.

B) The shahada
C) Charity
D) Jihad

Key Takeaway
The reward of an act is based on its benefit, not its difficulty.

The Ash'ari View on Miracles and the Philosophers' Classification
Ash'ari asserts miracles as divine acts; philosophers classify them into three, focusing on abstention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that miracles are direct divine acts requiring no natural preparedness, and the philosophers' initial classification of miracles into abstention, speech, and action, with elaboration on abstention.

Core Idea
Miracles are divine acts, not natural phenomena requiring preparation.

This passage introduces the second investigation into how miracles occur. The Ash'ari school affirms that miracles are acts of God, the free agent, manifested through prophets without any need for preparedness in the recipient. In contrast, the philosophers attempt to explain miracles through natural causes, dividing them into three categories: abstention, speech, and action. Here, we explore the first category, abstention, and its basis in the soul's purity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the contrast between Ash'ari and philosophers.
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Note that miracles are acts of the free agent (God) and require no preparedness in the recipient.
This is the central Ash'ari doctrine.
3
Understand the philosophers' classification
See that philosophers divide miracles into three types: abstention (tark), speech (qawl), and action (fiʿl). Focus on the first: abstention.
The passage only elaborates on abstention.
4
Examine the argument for abstention
The soul's purity leads to less need for nourishment, analogous to a sick person who abstains from food.
The analogy uses natural observation to support a miraculous occurrence.
5
Reflect on incompleteness
Note that the passage ends abruptly; only abstention is explained. Speech and action are not covered.
This may be addressed in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : في كيفية حصولها) المذهب (عندنا أنه فعل الفاعل المختار يظهرها على يد من يريد تصديقه بمشيئته لما تعلق به مشيئته ) من دعوى النبوة ممن أرسله إلى الناس ليدعوهم إلى ما ينجيهم ويسعدهم في الدارين ولا يشترط لإظهارها استعدادا كما لا يشترط في النبوة على ما خلافا للحكماء. ) وقال الفلاسفة : ( إنها ( تنقسم إلى ترك وقول وفعل أما الترك فمثل أن يمسك عن القوت المعتاد برهة من الزمان بخلاف العادة وسببه انجذاب النفس الزكية عن الكدورات البشرية إما لصفاء جوهرها في أصل فطرتها وإما لتصفيته بضرب من المجاهدة وقطع العلائق ) إلى عالم القدس واشتغالها ) بذلك ( عن تحليل مادة البدن فلا تحتاج إلى البدل كما نشاهده في المرضى من (أن النفس لاشتغالها بمقاومتها المرض ) من الأمراض الحادة وتحليلها للمواد الردية ( تنكف ) وتمتنع ( عن التحليل ) للمواد المحمودة ( فتمسك عن القوت ) الذي يحتاج إليه بدلاً عما تحلل من هذه المواد (ما لو أمسك ) أي زماناً لو أمسك عنه ( في ( أيام ( صحته شطره ) أي نصفه بل عشره (هلك ) بلا شبهة وإذا جاز ذلك في المريض كان جوازه في المتوجه المنخرط في سلك الملاء الأعلى أولى . وكيف لا ؟ والمرض مضاد للطبيعة ومضعف
The second investigation: on the manner of its occurrence. The doctrine with us is that it is an act of the free agent who manifests it upon the hand of whomever He wills to affirm through His will, for that which His will is attached to, of the claim of prophethood from whom He sent to people to call them to what saves them and makes them happy in the two abodes. And no preparedness is required for its manifestation, just as no preparedness is required for prophethood, contrary to the philosophers. And the philosophers said: it divides into abstention, speech, and action. As for abstention, like refraining from customary nourishment for a period of time, contrary to habit. Its cause is the attraction of the pure soul away from human impurities, either due to the purity of its substance in the origin of its nature, or due to its purification by a kind of struggle and cutting of attachments to the world of holiness, and its occupation with that from analyzing the body's matter, so it has no need for replacement, as we observe in the sick: that the soul, because of its occupation with resisting the disease from acute illnesses and analyzing the harmful materials, refrains and abstains from analyzing the praiseworthy materials, so it refrains from nourishment which it needs as a replacement for what has been analyzed from these materials. Such that if it had refrained, i.e., a time if it had refrained from it, during days of its health, for half of it, nay a tenth of it, it would perish without doubt. And if that is permissible in the sick, then its permissibility in the one who is turned towards and enrolled in the ranks of the highest assembly is more fitting. And how not? Illness is opposed to nature and weakening.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage begins a new investigation into how miracles occur (kayfiyat husuliha). The Ash'ari position (al-madhhab 'indana) is that the miracle is an act of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), i.e., God, who manifests it upon the hand of whomever He wills to affirm, without requiring any preparedness in the recipient, contrary to the philosophers. The philosophers' view is then introduced: they divide miracles into three types: abstention (tark), speech (qawl), and action (fiʿl). The passage elaborates only the first type: abstention, exemplified by miraculous fasting. Its cause is the soul's purity and detachment from bodily needs, similar to how a sick person may abstain from food. The argument is that if it is permissible for a sick person to abstain and survive, it is more fitting for a saintly person. However, the passage is cut off before completing the other types.

Key Terms
فعل الفاعل المختار
استعداد
ترك
قول
فعل
انجذاب النفس الزكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles require a natural preparedness or special purity in the recipient.
Correction: The Ash'ari view holds that God can create a miracle without any prior condition; it is purely His will.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position, what is required for the manifestation of a miracle?
A) Preparedness in the recipient
B) Divine will alone
C) Purity of the soul
D) A claim of prophethood

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school holds that miracles are unconditioned acts of God, while philosophers seek natural explanations, as seen in their account of miraculous fasting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something extraordinary in your life or others, do you instinctively look for a natural explanation or do you recognize the hand of God directly? What would change if you started seeing all good as a direct gift from Him, without any condition of worthiness?
?
The philosophers say that spiritual purity reduces physical needs. In your own pursuit of closeness to God, have you ever secretly trusted your own efforts more than His absolute power? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God can act without any preparedness from you?
?
If miracles require no prerequisite of purity or struggle, then why do you often hold back from turning to God in your deepest need, thinking you must 'get yourself right' first? What is one area of your life where you are waiting to be worthy instead of crying out to the One who can act freely at any moment?
