Body as Locus for the Soul's Preparedness
Argues that the body is a locus for the preparedness of the soul's attachment, not its essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the body can serve as a locus for the soul's preparedness for attachment and separation, without being a locus for the soul's own essence.

Core Idea
The body prepares for the soul's attachment, not for the soul's essence.

This passage tackles a key objection in philosophical theology: if the soul is immaterial, how can a physical body be involved in its existence? The author argues that the body is not a receptacle for the soul's essence, but rather a locus for the preparedness of its attachment and management. Through careful distinction, he shows that the body can be a locus for the soul's origination but not for its non-existence, thereby resolving the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the opening principle and how it is applied.
2
Identify the core principle
The passage begins with a claim about what can be a 'locus' (محل) for preparedness. Determine what is and what is not intelligible.
The principle distinguishes between what is 'essentially dependent' and what is 'of a different constitution'.
3
Apply to the rational soul
See how the principle applies to the rational soul. Even though the soul is immaterial, it is attached to the body for management. How does this make the body a locus?
Note the distinction between essential and accidental attribution of preparedness.
4
Distinguish between origination and non-existence
Follow the author's argument that the body can be a locus for preparedness of the soul's attachment (origination) but not for its non-existence. Understand why this distinction matters.
This distinction refutes the objection from 'absent forms' (الصور الغائبة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاستعداد وجود ما هو مباين القوام أو لاستعداد عدمه غير معقول بل الشيء إنما يكون محلاً لاستعداد وجود ما هو متعلق القوام به أي مستعد الوجود له ومحلاً لاستعداد فنائه أي مستعداً لعدمه عنه، فالنفس الناطقة وإن كانت مجردة في ذاتها لكنها متعلقة بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف لاستحصال كمالاتها بواسطته فيكون البدن محلاً لاستعداد تعلقها به وتصرفها فيه، ولما توقف تعلقها به على وجودها في نفسها كان هذا الاستعداد منسوباً أولاً وبالذات إلى تعلقها، وثانياً وبالعرض إلى وجودها في نفسها فهذا الاستعداد كاف لفيضان الوجود عليها متعلقة به ولا حاجة في ذلك إلى استعداد منسوب أولاً بالذات إلى وجودها في نفسها ليمتنع قيامه بالبدن لأنها من حيث وجودها في نفسها مباينة له والشيء لا يكون مستعداً لما هو مباين له، وكما جاز أن يكون البدن محلاً لاستعداد تعلقها به كذلك يجوز أن يكون محلاً لاستعداد انقطاع تعلقها به إذا خرجت عن المزاج الصالح لأن يكون محلاً لتدبيرها وتصرفها لكن لما لم يتوقف انقطاع تدبيرها على عدمها في نفسها لم يكن هذا الاستعداد منسوباً إلى عدمها في نفسها لا بالذات ولا بالعرض، فظهر الفرق بين استعداد حدوثها واستعداد عدمها وأن الأول يجوز قيامه بالبدن دون الثاني واندفع النقض بالصور الغائبة لأن انتفاء حلولها في المادة يستلزم انتفاءها عندهم وأنت خبير بأن هذا على بساطة النفس ،وتجردها وقد تحققت الآن وهذا الجواب الإجمالي والتفصيلي مذكور في المطولات.
For the preparedness of the existence of what is of a different constitution, or the preparedness of its non-existence, is not intelligible. Rather, a thing can only be a locus for the preparedness of the existence of what is essentially dependent on it – that is, prepared for its existence – and a locus for the preparedness of its perishing – that is, prepared for its non-existence from it. So the rational soul, even though it is immaterial in its essence, is nonetheless attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition in order to attain its perfections through it. Thus the body becomes a locus for the preparedness of its attachment to it and its disposition in it. And since its attachment to it depends on its existence in itself, this preparedness is attributed firstly and essentially to its attachment, and secondly and accidentally to its existence in itself. This preparedness is sufficient for the emanation of existence upon it while attached to it, and there is no need for a preparedness attributed firstly and essentially to its existence in itself, because its existence in itself prevents its subsistence in the body, since it is in its very existence different from it, and a thing is not prepared for what is different from it. And just as it is permissible for the body to be a locus for the preparedness of its attachment to it, so too it is permissible for it to be a locus for the preparedness of the severance of its attachment to it when it departs from the balanced temperament suitable for being a locus for its management and disposition. But since the severance of its management does not depend on its non-existence in itself, this preparedness is not attributed to its non-existence in itself, either essentially or accidentally. Thus the difference between the preparedness of its origination and the preparedness of its non-existence becomes apparent: the first may subsist in the body, but not the second. And the objection based on absent forms is refuted, because the non-existence of their immanence in matter entails their non-existence according to them. And you are aware that this is based on the simplicity and immateriality of the soul, which you have now realized. This summary and detailed answer is mentioned in the longer works.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument addressing an objection about how the soul can be prepared to exist and to separate from the body. It first asserts that it is unintelligible for a body to be a locus for the preparedness of something completely different in essence (مباين القوام). Instead, a body can only be a locus for the preparedness of something that is essentially dependent on it. The rational soul (النفس الناطقة), though immaterial, is attached to the body for management and disposition. The body thus becomes a locus for the preparedness of that attachment. This preparedness is attributed primarily to the attachment itself, and secondarily to the soul's existence in itself. The body need not be a locus for the soul's own essence, because the soul is in its very existence different from body. Similarly, the body can be a locus for the preparedness of the soul's detachment when the body loses its balanced temperament. However, this preparedness for detachment is not essentially or accidentally attributed to the soul's non-existence, because detachment does not entail the soul's annihilation. Thus there is a clear distinction between the preparedness for the soul's origination (which can subsist in the body) and the preparedness for its non-existence (which cannot). This refutes the objection from 'absent forms' (الصور الغائبة). The author concludes that this entire argument is based on the simplicity and immateriality of the soul, which has been established, and refers to longer works for a more detailed treatment.

Key Terms
استعداد
محل
مباينة
تعلق
تدبير
نفس ناطقة
تجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul's attachment to the body requires the body to be a receptacle for the soul's essence.
Correction: The body is only a locus for the preparedness of attachment, not for the soul's essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the preparedness for the soul's origination and the preparedness for its non-existence?
A) The first can subsist in the body, the second cannot.
B) The first is accidental, the second is essential.
C) The first is for abstract souls, the second for material souls.
D) There is no difference; both are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The body can be a locus for the preparedness of the soul's attachment but not for its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe in an immaterial soul. But when your body aches, does your soul feel pain, or do you forget that your body is merely a tool for your soul's perfection? In what way are you using your body today to attain the perfections of your soul, or are you letting your body’s desires hijack that purpose?
?
This lesson teaches that the body's preparedness for the soul's attachment is temporary, lasting only as long as the temperament is balanced. Are you living as if you have unlimited time to perfect your soul, or does the awareness that death severs this attachment change how you prioritize your life right now?
?
The passage implies that your soul's existence does not depend on your body, yet you are attached to this body for management. What would it mean for you to truly act as a manager (mudabbir) over your body rather than being managed by it? Is there one habit or attachment you need to sever today because it hinders your soul's perfection?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
First Mu'tazila Accusation: Denying Human Agency Closes the Proof for God
Refutes the claim that denying human agency closes the proof for the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that denying human agency undermines the proof for God's existence, and the Ash'ari response that there are alternative proofs.

Core Idea
Denying human agency does not close the door to proving the Creator.

The Mu'tazila accused the Ash'aris of disbelief for denying that humans are true agents of their actions. They argued that this denial destroys the primary method for proving God's existence—analogy from human actions to divine creation. In this passage, we see the charge and the Ash'ari rebuttal that other proofs exist.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila accusation
Read the passage to see what the Mu'tazila claimed and their reasoning. Note that they consider denial of human agency as disbelief because it supposedly closes the door to proving the Maker.
2
Identify the key analogy
The Mu'tazila rely on qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the witnessed to the absent). They argue that if human actions do not require human agency, the analogy for the world's need for a Creator fails.
This analogy is central to the Mu'tazila argument.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response
The author refutes the accusation by stating that the proof for the Creator is not limited to that analogy; there are five other proofs that do not depend on it. This shows that the accusation is invalid.
The five proofs are mentioned but not detailed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من تلك الأبحاث تكفير المعتزلة الأصحاب بأمور : الأول : إنكار كون العبد فاعلا لفعله لأنه يسد باب إثبات الصانع إذ طريقه قياس الغائب على الشاهد وإذا جاز عدم استناد فعلنا إلينا جاز استناد الحوادث لا إلى محدثها أي الطريق إلى احتياج العالم في حدوثه إلى الفاعل هو قياسه على حاجة أفعالنا في حدوثها إلينا فإذا لم تحتج في حدوثها إلينا، لم يمكن القياس، فالقول به سد لباب إثبات صانع العالم وهو كفر. قلنا : ليس الطريق إلى احتياج العالم في حدوثه إلى الصانع منحصراً في القياس المذكور، إذ قد تقدم لنا في إثبات الصانع وجوه خمسة لا يحتاج فيها إلى هذا القياس.
The second of those discussions: The declaration of disbelief by the Mu'tazila of the companions (al-ashab) in matters: The first: denial that the servant is an agent of his action, because it closes the door of proving the Maker, since the method for that is analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the absent (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). If it is permissible for our action not to be attributed to us, then it is permissible for events not to be attributed to their originator. That is, the way to establish the need of the world in its origination for the agent is by analogy to the need of our actions in their origination for us. So if we do not need them in their origination, the analogy cannot be made. So holding this view closes the door of proving the Maker of the world, and that is disbelief. We said: The way to the need of the world in its origination for the Maker is not restricted to that analogy, for we have previously presented five proofs for proving the Maker that do not need this analogy.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents the first accusation from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'aris (referred to as 'al-ashab' – the companions). The Mu'tazila argue that denying human agency (i.e., the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb) closes the door to proving the existence of God. Their reasoning is that the only way to prove the Creator is by analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid): just as our actions require an agent, so do the events of the world. If human actions do not require human agency, then the analogy collapses, and one cannot prove the Creator, which constitutes disbelief. The response from the Ash'ari side denies that this is the only method; they claim to have five other proofs for the existence of God that do not rely on this analogy. Thus, the accusation fails.

Key Terms
تكفير
فعل العبد
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
إثبات الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only way to prove God's existence is through analogy from human agency (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid).
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that there are five other proofs for the Creator that do not rely on this analogy, so denying human agency does not necessarily close the door to proving God.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why does denying human agency not necessarily close the door to proving the Creator?
A) Because human agency is proven by revelation
B) Because there are five other proofs for the Creator that do not rely on analogy
C) Because the analogy is only for prophets
D) Because the Mu'tazila themselves accept other proofs

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila accusation fails because the Ash'aris have multiple proofs for God's existence, not solely the analogy from human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the only proof you had for God's existence were the analogy from human actions, and that analogy were challenged, would your faith be shaken? This lesson shows that the Ash'aris had multiple proofs. Do you personally rely on a single argument, or have you built a robust foundation? What would it take for you to explore other proofs so that your conviction is not vulnerable to one refutation?
?
The Mu'tazila were quick to declare disbelief based on their methodological assumptions. Have you ever accused someone of being misguided or faithless because they held a different theological reasoning? Reflect on the manners of the Ash'aris: they responded with evidence rather than counter-accusation. Is there someone you need to approach with more humility and intellectual charity?
?
You have heard that there are 'five proofs' for the Creator. Do you know what they are? If not, this lesson reveals your own potential neglect of the intellectual heritage of Islam. Will you commit to studying at least one of those proofs this week so that your faith is not based on a single argument?

Refuting Takfir: The Mu'tazilite Argument on Human Agency
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that denying human agency closes the door to proving the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying human agency does not necessarily lead to denying the existence of God, and how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim.

Core Idea
Denying human agency does not close the door to proving the Creator.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a key accusation from the Mu'tazila: that if humans are not the real agents of their actions, then the analogical argument for God's existence collapses, leading to atheism. He counters by noting that the proof for God is not limited to that one analogy; there are multiple independent proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own argument flow through.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
The passage presents an objection. Find the reasoning: what do the Mu'tazila claim follows from denying human agency? How do they link it to disbelief?
They claim the only method to prove a Creator is analogy from human actions.
3
Find Sanusi's response
Notice how Sanusi refutes the charge. What does he say about the method of proving the Creator?
He says there are five proofs that do not rely on that analogy.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this matters for the debate on free will and theological disagreement. Does this argument justify takfir?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من تلك الأبحاث تكفير المعتزلة الأصحاب بأمور : الأول : إنكار كون العبد فاعلا لفعله لأنه يسد باب إثبات الصانع إذ طريقه قياس الغائب على الشاهد وإذا جاز عدم استناد فعلنا إلينا جاز استناد الحوادث لا إلى محدثها أي الطريق إلى احتياج العالم في حدوثه إلى الفاعل هو قياسه على حاجة أفعالنا في حدوثها إلينا فإذا لم تحتج في حدوثها إلينا، لم يمكن القياس، فالقول به سد لباب إثبات صانع العالم وهو كفر، قلنا : ليس الطريق إلى احتياج العالم في حدوثه إلى الصانع منحصراً في القياس المذكور، إذ قد تقدم لنا في إثبات الصانع وجوه خمسة لا يحتاج فيها إلى هذا القياس.
The second: From these discussions [regarding] takfir of the Mu'tazila the companions by matters: The first: Denial that the servant is an agent of his action, because it closes the door to proving the Maker, since its method is analogy from the witnessed to the absent. And if it is permissible that our actions do not depend on us, it is permissible that events do not depend on their originator. That is, the way to the world's need in its origination for an agent is analogizing it to the need of our actions in their origination to us. So if [our actions] do not need us in their origination, then analogy is not possible. So holding this view closes the door to proving the Maker of the world, and that is disbelief. We say: The way to the world's need in its origination for the Maker is not limited to the mentioned analogy, for we have previously presented in proving the Maker five proofs that do not require this analogy.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila that if humans are not agents of their actions, then the analogical argument for a Creator (from human actions to divine creation) fails, thus leading to atheism and disbelief. The answer is that the proof for the Creator does not rely solely on that analogy; there are other independent proofs (five mentioned earlier) that do not need it. Therefore, the charge of disbelief is invalid.

Key Terms
العبد فاعل
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
إثبات الصانع
وجوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only way to prove the existence of a Creator is through analogy from human actions (قياس الغائب على الشاهد).
Correction: There are multiple independent proofs for the Creator; the analogy is not the only method.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite argument for takfir in this passage?
A) Denial of human agency leads to denial of prophethood
B) Denial of human agency closes the only method to prove the Creator
C) Denial of human agency implies God is unjust
D) Denial of human agency contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument for takfir based on denial of human agency is invalid because the proof of a Creator does not depend solely on analogy from human acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a single argument for your faith, such that when it was challenged, you felt your faith shake? This lesson shows that the proof of God does not depend on one analogy. Where is your trust really placed—in the strength of an argument or in the reality of the Creator Himself?
?
The Mu'tazilites were willing to excommunicate those who disagreed with them on human agency. Are there doctrines you hold so tightly that you would cut off a fellow believer? How does this passage challenge the spirit of takfir within your own heart?
?
You believe that God is the Creator of all things, including your actions. But do you truly live as though your actions are your own responsibility, or do you fall into passivity? This lesson separates the theological debate from your daily accountability. How does understanding that human agency is not a condition for proving God change how you approach your own actions before Him?

Causality and Created Things

Refuting the Objection on Actions and Miracles
Sanusi presents and refutes the objection that attributing all actions to God makes miracles unreliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing all actions to God does not undermine miracles or prophethood, and how the concept of 'customary continuity' (adah) preserves the evidential value of miracles.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond good and evil; miracles are safeguarded by custom, not by impossibility.

A key objection against Ash'ari causality is that if God creates all actions, including evil ones, then miracles could be performed by liars, breaking the link between miracles and prophethood. Sanusi presents this objection and then refutes it by arguing that ugliness does not apply to God and that miracles are preserved by customary continuity. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer introduced by 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the objection's two consequences
The objection claims that if God does ugly acts, then (1) a liar could receive a miracle, and (2) lying about God becomes possible, destroying trust in His speech. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the two-part answer
The answer has two parts: (a) ugliness does not apply to God, so the premise is false; (b) even if a liar's miracle were rationally possible, it is known not to occur by custom (adah). Reflect on how each part refutes the objection.
The second part relies on the concept of customary continuity discussed earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من تلك الأمور نسبة فعل العبد إلى الله تعالى فلانه يلزمه كونه فاعلاً للقبائح فجاز حينئذ إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب، إذ غايته أنه فعل قبيح وقد جوزتم صدوره عنه تعالى فلا يبقى للمعجزة دلالة على صدق النبي وجاز أيضاً الكذب عليه سبحانه فيرتفع الوثوق عن كلامه في وعده ووعيده وفيه إبطال الشرائع بالكلية، قلنا : قد أجبنا عنه بما مر من أنه لا قبيح بالنسبة إليه تعالى بل الأفعال كلها يحسن صدورها عنه، ومن أن إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب وإن كان ممكناً صدوره عنه عقلاً إلا أنه معلوم انتفاؤه عادة كسائر العاديات إلى آخر ما مر في البحث عن كيفية دلالة المعجزة.
The second: From those matters is attributing the action of the servant to God Almighty, because it necessitates Him being the doer of ugly acts. Then it would be permissible to manifest a miracle at the hand of a liar, since its ultimate is that it is an ugly act, and you have permitted it to occur from Him, so the miracle no longer indicates the truthfulness of the prophet. And it would also be permissible to lie about Him, so trust in His speech regarding His promise and threat is lost, and this entails nullifying the laws entirely. We said: We have already answered this with what has passed: that there is no ugliness with respect to Him, rather all actions are good coming from Him; and that manifesting a miracle at the hand of a liar, although possible in reason, is known to not occur customarily, like other customary matters, as passed in the discussion on the modality of the miracle's indication.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: if God creates all actions, including human evil acts, then God does ugly acts, which would break the link between miracles and truthfulness (since a liar could also get a miracle), and would lead to distrust in God's promises. The answer reiterates that ugliness is not applicable to God; all His actions are good. As for miracles appearing via liars, it is rationally possible but known not to happen due to customary continuity (adah), as previously discussed.

Key Terms
القبيح
المعجزة
العادة
الإبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, including evil, then miracles could be performed by false prophets, so we cannot trust any miracle as a sign of truth.
Correction: The author explains that ugliness does not apply to God, so the objection is invalid. Additionally, miracles via liars are known not to occur by custom, so miracles still reliably indicate truthfulness.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the argument that attributing all actions to God would allow miracles to appear via liars?
A) By denying that miracles can occur via liars
B) By stating that it is rationally impossible
C) By saying that it is known not to occur customarily
D) By claiming that God never does ugly acts

Key Takeaway
Attributing all actions to God does not undermine miracles because God's actions are beyond moral categories, and miracles are safeguarded by customary continuity, not rational impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you hide behind the thought that 'God created this action, so it's not really me'? The lesson says God's actions are beyond blame, but yours are not. How will you take full ownership of your sins today and repent, rather than shifting responsibility?
?
If you truly believed that God's promises are as certain as the sun rising (because of customary continuity), would you worry less about your future? Will you consciously affirm that trust in Him is not based on rational proof but on His consistent habit, and commit to acting on that certainty in one specific decision this week?
?
This lesson shows that even a rationally possible event (a liar's miracle) is rejected by custom. Are there 'rational possibilities' you entertain to doubt God's truth? What would it look like for you to submit your intellect to God's customary way and stop seeking loopholes?
